While it's highly unlikely, it is technically possible to hatch a quail chick from a Tesco (or any supermarket) egg, including those from a Tesco quail egg box, but the odds are very low. The vast majority of eggs sold in supermarkets are not fertilized, meaning they cannot develop into chicks. However, some commercial farms do allow a small number of roosters with their hens, and very rarely, a fertilized egg can end up in the supply chain

Sainsbury's or any other company could not care less whether you hatched the eggs or found a lizard in one. Their only interest is to SELL the eggs after buying them as cheaply as possible from the suppliers. However, a fertile egg may survive in a dormant state for up to 10 days, after that it will die, in most cases, a fertile egg left alone without heat or turning will not survive. While a fertilized egg can remain dormant for a period, it needs consistent warmth and turning to develop and hatch.   Supermarket eggs come from mainly poultry houses which produce infertile eggs, there are no male birds there and the hens have a life from hell in confined cages, the eggs are laid into a sloping channel where they roll down to a collecting point. Hens will produce infertile eggs for a long time.  . The eggs you find in grocery stores are almost always infertile. This video is therefore suspect.

well, most of the time, these eggs are not even gonna turn into a chicken even if we didnt eat them, chickens randomly lay infertile eggs everyday, which are the eggs we take. the eggs detected to be fertile, are forced to be infertile, by first letting them sit there in the cold. like the professional said, some supermarkets dont consider making the eggs infertile, meaning if your quick enough, you can hatch them.
Umm, ex home chicken breeder here... you should turn off the egg rotation function of the incubator (should have a simple switch and be detailed in its instructions) about 2 or 3 days before the estimated hatching date (textbooks will tell the right time,   it varies for different species. of birds).Â   Normally leave the first hatchlings inside the incubator for at least 24 hours to allow any slightly slower hatchlings to emerge. Chicks don't need anything to eat or drink for more than the first 24 hours. This gives time for the fluff and pre-feathers to thoroughly dry and fluff up. Â   Waterfowl eggs, such as ducks and geese etc, should be kept moister. Some incubators will have different settings for putting more, or less,  water in them, for different birds.  Normal supermarket hen eggs will come from hen only farms.Â   Quail eggs are more likely to come from a farm including quail-roosters. Â   Fertile eggs kept in fridge temperatures or even cool unheated room temperatures should still be good for one to 2 weeks. Lack of occasional rotation or movement can be more an issue.Â Strictly speaking eggs "should" be stored lying down. However if you're using a typical "dozen" egg carton, then canting it at an angle, first raising at one end, and then at the other, by setting one end atop another carton, will give enough rotation movement for the initial pre-incubation storage time.   Remember in the wild the female bird lays one egg each day or each second day, then covers and abandons the nest, until ready to go back and add another egg. Lain eggs cool off in the nest and enter a state of suspended animation.  Once the female bird feels like she has a reasonable  number of eggs under her bum, whatever she feels comfortable keeping warm, (probably 5 to 10) then she will keep sitting and start to incubate them.  Eggs can cool off very slightly without harm. Mother hens leave the nest at least once a day for a few minutes to drink water, and if food is easy to find, they may have a quick bite to eat. Â   When I lived in rural Australia sometimes it was VERY hot, even in the shade (35*C plus).  On very hot days, even in a shaded old metal shed/barn, I sometimes saw mother hens sitting on zero eggs, with their eggs arranged in a circle around them, just beyond their body. This was to stop the eggs overheating, while remaining under mother's watchful eye. As it cooled off in late afternoon and early evening, they would roll the eggs back underneath themselves again.    Some incubators come with detailed instructions but otherwise buy a textbook on Incubation. Nowadays it's probably all on that newfangled internetty thing eh ?

What do you do with them after video ends? To anyone who reads this: PLEASE do not hatch the eggs on your own! theres something very irresponsible about these types of videos and I just cant shake it. I mean all of these birds ended up in an animal sanctuary which is great for the birds but not so good for sanctuaries as it means they're less able to take in animals which are actually in need. plus he is not "saving'' these eggs, yes they were fertilised but that just means they had the potential to become a chick, not that he somehow saved a load of quails by doing this. instead he just added a bunch more quails to the world which I dont really think is needed. it would be different if he had intended to keep these birds but obviously thats not what happened. I just dont think adding more animals to the world and dumping them on a sanctuary is a very responsible thing to do for an experiment for a youtube video. and it encourages his audience to do the same, which I sincerely hope nobody does unless they actually know what they're doing and intend to keep the birds at the end of it. Basically if you didnt already want quails, dont do this.  I dont think there were bad intentions making this video and I can tell he did care about the animals as he did actually give them proper care and made sure they had somewhere to go when he no longer could take care of them. but I just think the premise of this as an experiment is rather irresponsible.

I remember how excited I felt when I hatched my first batch of chicken eggs. And I still get excited with every batch I hatch. I have been raising and intubating my own eggs for almost 5 yrs now. I haven't bought eggs ever since. If my eggs aren't fertile, then we eat them and sell them. If they r fertile, we incubated and either keep them, sell them, or sometimes give them away. But remember, if u want fertilized eggs, then u need a hen and a rooster. If you just want eggs to eat, you only need a hen. Also, I forgot to say I have different breeds of chickens and even ducks. And yes, you can eat duck eggs. Duck eggs are best for baking. They make your cakes, bread, cookies. Whatever ur baking duck eggs make them so much fluffier. And they make great omletes.

I didnâ€™t watch the vid but this title alone is stupid because it is quite literally impossible to hatch these supermarket eggs because they are not fertilized. Eggs in stores come from hens (female chickens) and for them to hatch they must be fertilized by a rooster (male chicken) but, the hens that produce these eggs that go to stores do not ever meet roosters so it is impossible to fertilize the eggs and therefore impossible to hatch them. Again I didnâ€™t watch the video I only saw the title but if the eggs actually did end up hatching Iâ€™m sure sometime they were swapped out with fertilized eggs when he wasnâ€™t filming. 1. It's extremely common for quail eggs to be fertilized as there are less restrictions (it's a specialty food item, and not really "eaten for breakfast" as they're quite small) 2. Fertilized eggs does not mean there is a bird, it just means it's fertilized. I dunno why this dude puts such emphasis on it like it's a serious problem. Farm fresh eggs from markets are also often fertilized.  3. Supermarket chicken eggs (the ones you DO eat for breakfast) are never fertilized compared to quail eggs.  4. Future reference, don't leave older chicks with younger chicks because they will peck/bully the smaller ones and even kill them. Quail are monstrous to other quail.  5. You keep conflating chicken statistics with quail. I've no doubt many chicken chicks die every year, but you hatched quails buddy.  6. Quail eggs have more vitamins and minerals proportionately than chicken eggs (and are very good pickled :) ) not a correction, just an interesting fact.  7. If you DO do this yourself - be sure to get a 30% protein feed, as quail need much higher protein than chickens do in order to keep themselves going. DO NOT feed them chicken crumb like this nob did.

Every quail farm know there're some Male quails that can be look like hens and some hens can look like Males. It can't be 100% check by colours or strips on the chest, only blood check for 100% which's cost a lot for 1 bird. It's common that there will be some male mix in the cage and because the battery cage are banned in UK. So they keep them all in the large cage. And 1 Male can mat with 10 hens, so it's not about the corruption. It's about control & cost issue. You probably made some farmers lost their business. Congrats!

Few tips for curious minds and potential quail owners: 1) something might go wrong during hatching, so be morally ready for it. 2) chicks mortality rate is high and quails are not the strongest bird in the world, be ready for that as well. ğŸ«¡ 3) quails donâ€™t really like open spaces much. They associate it with being out in the open for potential flying predators. But it doesnâ€™t mean all chicks are like that. Some will do just fine hanging out with their owners.

pretty cool. just means the farm the eggs came from had a rooster. Regular factory farm chicken eggs off of the shelf will not do this because there is no rooster to fertilize the eggs. Small batch quail egg farms probably just take the chance that you are going to refrigerate them/eat them before this happens. Fertilized eggs can sit on a shelf for a month and then if you incubate them they will start the process of making a baby chicken. neat stuff.

Refrigeration does not necessarily 'kill' the egg. If they are refrigerated at an early growth stage, and then gradually brought back to room temperature before being incubated, they are still likely to be viable. If they are 'kept' refrigerated (more than a week or so), it will kill the egg.

I believe the lady was talking about chicken eggs, Chicken Hens will freely lay out of the presence of male birds. As far as I know Quail are kept in large flocks of mixed sexes because the females are stimulated to lay by the presence of male birds and so the majority of Quail eggs sold in markets are going to be fertile. Side note for anyone keeping quail, they are extremely nervous birds and easily startled. There first instinct when startled is usually to fly upward to escape whatever has frightened them. You have to either house them in a pen less than 10 inches (25.4 cm)  high or higher than about 4.5 feet (137 cm), because anything in between those heights lets the birds try to fly up and they often hurts themselves hitting the roof of the pen and can even break their own necks. Glad the quail are doing well, and glad you did this lovely experiment. Good job, Happily Subbed. <3

I live in Guatemala and I am pretty sure most of the eggs I eat are fertilized. Correct me if I am mistaken, but fertilized eggs have a prominent dot on the yoke. Eggs are super food, and you can pick up chicks at any feed store. This was  definitely a surprise to see someone hatch grocery store eggs.
193 out of 350 that's about 55% hatch quota. For purchased eggs, that's a pretty good. Here in Germany, you can assume that most quail eggs you buy are fertilized. It is, however, not recommended to blindly buy quail eggs. I'm not sure how it is in UK, but in the EU, there are no real regulations about farming quails. While there are laws in place on how to keep chickens, there aren't for quails, so they often live under horrible conditions. They're also often wrongly declared. The farms write "free range", although they're actually battery-caged, because the terms aren't regulated for quails either. I'm not saying don't buy quail eggs at all, they actually have some benefits to chicken eggs. You may need to eat more quail eggs for one chicken egg, but they're higher in cholesterol (the good one) and have more beneficial fatty acids, vitamins and iron (they're also creamier and a hard-boiled quail egg is a perfect bite-sized snack). But if you do buy them, make sure they come from a place where the quails are kept under appropriate conditions.

I have a major problem with what you did.  You hatch out a lot of chicks that you never intended to keep and then dump them at an animal sanctuary.  It is the same as letting you cat or dog have babies and then dumping them on your friends.  If you never intended to keep them, what is the point of hatching them out?  They NEVER would have been slaughtered for dog food.  They were eggs intended for humans to eat.  They were POTENTIAL quail, they were the equivalent of seeds.  So, out of 350 edible eggs you wasted 157 eggs and possibly got a lot of farmers in trouble for trying to keep up with inflexible quotas to grocery stores and restaurants.   If people want to hatch out quail BUY FROM A REPUTABLE BREEDER.  That helps quail breeders stay in business and protects egg farmers from YouTubers like you.

Great video and loved how excited you were through the whole process. Just a couple of points, you can do this with most (organic) eggs including Quail, Chicken and Duck because they often have roosters as guards against foxes. The fertilised eggs are perfectly safe to eat and leaving them in your cupboard will not make them hatch as they temp and humidity needs to be just right. They say to refrigerate them as they will have been washed which removes the protective bloom and they will go off quicker if left in a warm room. Also you are meant to turn off the automatic turners 3 days before they hatch so for Quail day 15, Chickens day 18 and Ducks day 25... just for next time ;)

I really don't know why anyone hasn't said this yet under any of these youtube videos on people successfully hatching supermarket bought eggs:  All these videos are done by people in the UK or EUROPE. You canNOT do this in the USA or Canada, because the way the eggs are processed there essentially kills the chick before it ever reaches the store in the first place. Hence why there isn't a SINGLE youtube video of this experiment done in these 2 countries,  So, at least if you're American or Canadian, since idk abt other countries besides USA Canada or European countries, please don't watch these videos and then be inspired to try this yourself, and then get puzzled at why it never seems to work, but these youtubers seem to have some crazy magic voodoo luck or something. No. It simply won't happen if you are in USA or Canada. it seems to work, only because the people who successfully do it are from countries where the processing methods happen to allow such experiments to be possible. I have nothing against this youtuber, but theres too many viewers out there who think its so tragic that there are so many chickens who could have had a second chance at life, and they want to do it so bad.

If the eggs are fertilized, they already contain life, you're not creating life by hatching them, you're just helping it along.  But commercial chicken farms are very strict about not having any roosters in with the hens, so the eggs are not fertilized. They are more like a chicken's period than a baby chick. Also, the yoke is not the baby chick, it is the food sack that the chick gets it's nutrients from.  My parents have a small farm and have both hens and roosters and they often let some eggs hatch to replace laying hens (which get turned into stewing hens) and get some young roosters for frying chickens (the breading rooster always comes from another farm so you don't get inbreeding). I personally don't have any issues with eating eggs, even if I know they are fertilized. I have more of an issue with eating a stewing hen that I have gotten to know through her years of egg laying.  Though, they also have a couple cows at a time (often rescued from dairy farms where they get cows pregnant just to start their milk production, then they dispose of the calves). They hand raise them and name them and you get to know their personalities... then, every Christmas, we get a half a cow (butchered and packaged to be frozen for a full year's worth of beef). The cows are pampered and live their best life before they are slaughtered. I always think that, if they were living in the wild, they would probably be taken down by a pack of wolves or a bear and it would be a much worse way to go than a shot that they can't even feel. But it's still weird when you think of the cow's name as you eat it. Luna was a cow that they let loose in their apple orchard all the time because she LOVED apples (she'd walk up on her hind legs to pick apples off the trees). It would seem like you could almost taste a bit of a hint of apple in the beef.  I think that all the animals that we eat should have as great a life as possible. Most animals rely on other animals for their survival, including humans. Our bodies are designed to digest meat and our taste buds like the taste of it so much because our body needs it. You'd never put a dog or cat on a vegan diet and call it humane treatment, so why do people do it to humans?

So just to ease your conscious a little, bird eggs are laid in a "dormant" state. what that means is that the embryo does not begin to develop until the incubation heat is applied. So when you eat eggs for breakfast, you are eating something that has the potential to become a chick, but it is not yet a chick until incubation begins.

This was an amazing watch.  Also brings to light the differences in grocery practice between where you are and Canada (where I am).  In Canada, all eggs in grocery stores are not only initially kept refrigerated in the shops, but they're also candled and pasteurized in the packaging process.  It is exceedingly rare for an egg to develop after all that, and when one does manage it unfortunately it's even more rare for them to live for very long or be discovered before perishing inside the egg.  Because of the pasteurizing process the eggs go bad faster outside of refrigeration, so most people just automatically put the pack in the fridge. Very cool, I hope you don't run into any legal trouble for this experiment!

I don't know if it's been said here. But to the people worried about eating eggs that may be able to produce a live chick, just dont eat range eggs or most so called organic eggs.or quail eggs. Most of your supermarket eggs are from factories where there are no Roosters, wich is required to fertilize each egg.So no worries.  If you crack a fresh egg and there is a tiny red speck inside, that is the Roosters part,it has been fertilized. But it's no big deal, you can still eat it. I have my own chickens with Roosters present, and eat plenty of them. The main thing is to always put all your eggs into a small  container of water if they sink like a rock they are fresh and good to eat. If they float they are bad. If maybe they tip upright but not float they are still good but not very fresh. No worries people.  And oh yes hatching out chicks is an amazing thing to experience. The gift of life in all things is wonderful.

Two notes for someone that raises quail. To improve your hatch rate, turn off the turner at 14 days, the chicks need a few days to move into hatching position and they can become confused and not move into hatching position if the turner is left on after day 14. Also they can hatch as late at day 23 so you should have left the incubator on lock down until you were sure no more eggs would hatch - especially with a small home incubator that likely had hot and cold spots. Also leave the chicks in the incubator for their first day, their chirping encourages the other eggs to hatch and gives the babies a chance to dry off - you moved the two babies to the brooder a bit too early, thankfully they seemed to pull through, but they can get too cold a die doing that, but I bet more eggs would have hatched if you had let the incubator run longer.

Hell Jay, Im a tractor supply company team leader and I wanted to give you a little advice on potential fore comings as this is the time of year when our company sells baby chicks. You are going to inevitably have some chicks die along the way however they are things you can prevent as they happen, one big one being compaction or pasting up of the  cloaca. This happens when the excrement of the baby birds builds up around the outside of cloaca mixing with dirt and other debris and prevents further excrement from being able to exit their bodies. If you notice that a chick is pasting up underneath its tail feathers, you should gently hold the chick on under warm running water, not hot. As the water begins to soften it you can gently use a finger to rub in a circular motion  in aid of removing the excess debris and stimulating the area around the cloaca enough to remove anything that is fully hardened in the opening. If done correctly the chick should soon after pass a large excrement.
